The truce called after the fall of the Accelerated Department has not stopped minor skirmishes between Sichuan's military factions. This has motivated President Zhang to undertake significant efforts to coordinate the civilian and military officials as a more united movement.\n\nIn a move to portray unity, Prime Minister Xiong Kewu has affirmed the President's reforms, declaring them to be beneficial to the stability of [ROOT.GetNameDef]. Premier of the Hour
While President Zhang has quickly emerged as the face of our Federalist Movement, those who partake in the National Reconstruction Government know that it is Prime Minister Xiong Kewu who holds it all together. A veteran of the Xinhai Revolution, National Protection War, and a slew of internal provincial conflicts, Xiong Kewu emerged as a key member of the Federalist Movement during the Warlord Era. During the Zhili Hegemony, Xiong found himself forced from the pentarchy established by Yang Sen, Liu Xiang, and the Baoding Department Triumvirate.\n\nHowever, after Yang faced a challenge from Liu Xiang's wing of the Accelerated Department, Xiong was able to capitalise on the provincial infighting to once again emerge as Sichuan's pre-eminent warlord. As Prime Minister of the Sichuan Federalist Government, Xiong has integrated his 9-Man Regiment faction with the Grand Republican Alliance, a coalition party composed of various primarily southern parties opposed to the Beiyang order which has operated as the chief Federalist political organisation since 1931.\n\nWhile Xiong has concentrated his efforts on keeping the Federal Government united under himself and Zhang Lan, he has had Dan Maoxin utilised their shared influence in the GRA to keep its internal factions as aligned as possible to the National Reconstruction Government's interests, and has leveraged their political support to keep the other warlords from gaining too much influence in the Zhang Lan Administration.
We're lucky to have Xiong Kewu at the helm!
Remind us why he's in charge again?
The Broken Triumvirate
While Prime Minister Xiong Kewu and President Zhang Lan have taken centre stage of the new National Reconstruction Government, in their shadow remains the ever-resilient Baoding Department. Surviving the decade-long struggle within Sichuan Province during the Warlord Era, the Baoding Department was able to secure an accord with Yang Sen's Accelerated Department during the decade of Zhili Hegemony. Throughout the decade, the Baoding Department under Deng Xihou, Tian Songyao, and Liu Wenhui were able to become one of the major factions within Sichuan's political structure, becoming three of Sichuan's \
What utter nonsense is this?
Nono, he makes a good point.
Rising Tides of the Federalist Movement
While many assumed that the Federalists could not come back from the loss of Yunnan and Guangdong, President Zhang Lan and Prime Minister Xiong Kewu have proved them all wrong. In a shocking twist of events, the National Reconstruction Government based in Sichuan was able to break the stronghold of the Beiyang Warlords, putting Beijing in the hands of a southern-led government, a feat not even the Xinhai Revolutionaries could achieve.\n\nArriving in Beijing to a jubilant crowd, Xiong Kewu declared to the masses that this would only be the start of the National Reconstruction Government's unification campaign. While the National Reconstruction is undoubtedly the strongest government claiming to represent China, it must see the nation entirely united before it claims true success. As President Zhang Lan organises Beijing's new government, Premier Xiong Kewu prepares the military for China's final struggle.
Our Movement restores order wherever we march!
We bring freedom to our beloved Republic!
Victory for National Reconstruction
Only a few years prior, it seemed that democracy in China was on the cusp of extinction, overrun either by opportunistic reactionaries or revolutionary autocrats. However, today the Federal Government of China stretches from the frigid Northeast to the mountains of Guangxi. Although rightfully recognised as a triumphant victory of both the China Democratic League and the Grand Republican Alliance, the government is both paradoxically at its zenith, as well as overlooking the precipice of a mounting crisis.\n\nWhile none can deny that the Federalist Government controls most of China, victory has whittled away at the unity of Zhang Lan's government. Xiong Kewu's plethora of GRA allies are unlikely to remain content to remain in the shadow of Zhang Lan and the CDL, and the President must work to secure his gains or see the federal government trip at perhaps its most crucial hour.
Unification was secured by the idealistic resolve of President Zhang!
Xiong Kewu is the sword of victory for our movement!
Cruel Realities of the Military
While the Federal military began composed of a coalition of warlord forces, Zhang's governmental reforms and an impressive amount of luck kept the Federal military largely intact during the Chinese Civil War and War of Resistance against Japan. However, with unification finally having been undertaken, the fractious army has finally begun fully breaking down. Although President Zhang Lan has proposed military demobilisation to deal with the bloated military force, he has found difficulty in persuading the military to go along with it. Due to the high prevalence of warlord influence in his government, Zhang Lan has found great difficulty in regulating the Federal military.\n\nPrime Minister Xiong Kewu, keen on exploiting the crisis, has criticised the President for fracturing the hard-fought unity of the Federal Government. With the underlying tensions between the Prime Minister and the President finally boiling over, there have been rumours of a potential coup being formulated by disgruntled military officers, possibly led by Liu Wenhui. Liu for his part has reframed from outright criticising the President, and has taken a more ambivalent stance towards the demobilisation campaign. To outside observers, it has become apparent that the Zhang Lan government and its grand coalition has become untenable.
Xiong is able to strong-arm the President into delaying demobilisation.
President Zhang stands firm against the Prime Minister.
Crippling Economic Downturns
While China once boasted one of the world's largest economies, the Century of Humiliation has relegated our crippled nation to one of the most undeveloped economies in the world. While coastal regions saw modest growth during the Zhili Hegemony, the agrarian hinterland remained in a backwards state. Whatever economic reform undertaken by the various provincial warlords have seen what little progress existed destroyed by the near-constant state of warfare China found itself in following the collapse of the League in 1936, and the War of Resistance against the Empire of Japan caused the Chinese economy to enter into a state of hyperinflation. With paper money quickly becoming worthless, people have begun to use a crude system of bartering, until the government is able to stem the developing economic crisis.
Premier Xiong cracks down on criminal gangs for hoarding resources.
President Zhang introduces the public distribution of commodities.
National Reconstruction, Fraying At Its Seams
The Sichuan Federalist Government was remarkable for its level of success in unifying China, despite the mutual hostility of the various factions within the Zhang Lan Administration. The coming elections announced by the Federal Government have not helped in mending the fraying government. As election campaigning intensifies, partisanship has only become more rife within Zhang Lan's administration.\n\nThe President, to the surprise of many, has declared his intent to run for re-election. This move was met with a mixed reaction within China Democratic League, with many prominent members of the CDL criticising the move. However, unable to agree on any other suitable candidate, the party has largely fallen behind Zhang's Presidential campaign.\n\nMeanwhile within the Grand Republican Alliance's camp, Premier Xiong has largely pulled out of active governance, focusing on the electoral campaign. With Xiong still refusing to run as President, the GRA campaign has focused on making Recognising that a Presidential run under his name may be marred by perceptions of it being a blatant power grab, Xiong has pushed his right-hand Dan Maoxin to be the GRA Presidential candidate.\n\nIn Northern China, Liu Wenhui has found a surprising amount of supporters, especially amongst Ex-Beiyang officers who see him as a symbol of stable governance. However, Liu Wenhui has thus far refused to overtly run for President, instead hoping to be made Minister of War of the electoral victor, whoever that ends up being.
This election solves nothing!
May the best man win.
National Reconstruction Falters
With relations between President Zhang Lan and Prime Minister Xiong Kewu at an alltime low, the National Reconstruction Government finds itself in a uniquely weak position. With China once again engulfed in another political crisis, perhaps it falls to [ROOT.GetLeader] to pick up the pieces and stake his claim on the Republic, and save it from this motley bunch of squabbling fools\n\n[ROOT.GetLeader] understands the risk in revolting, but he also understands that there may never be a better opportunity. However, [ROOT.GetLeader] also knows that challenging the Federal Government may be regarded by the people of China as nothing more than a selfish warlord attempting to once again plunge the nation into chaos, and result in him being remembered as one of the nation's greatest villains for ten thousand years.
[ROOT.GetLeader] is the only man suited to save the Republic!

[FROM.GetLeader] Strikes Out!
With the schism between Xiong Kewu's Grand Republican Alliance and Zhang Lan's China Democratic League growing larger by the day, the disintegrating unity of the National Reconstruction Government has left ambitious warlords and governors with a small window of opportunity to strike.\n\nIndeed, it seems that [FROM.GetLeader] in particular has seized upon the opportunity, declaring President Zhang Lan and Premier Xong Kewu's infighting to be plaguing the Republican government, and has declared himself as the head of a provisional government that will end infighting in China.

Retreat of the Iron-Footed General
After a long period of increasing tensions in the National Reconstruction Government, Prime Minister Xiong has formally declared his exit from the Zhang Lan Administration. Following this, a slew of Grand Republican Alliance-aligned officials also tendered their resignations. The mass exit has left a substantial power vacuum within the government, with many in the China Democratic League attempting to fill in the vacant positions. Perhaps at the centre of the most intrigue in the Zhang Lan Administration is the Premiership.\n\nTacitly understood that the succeeding Prime Minister would likely be retained by Zhang after the election, many high ranking members of the CDL have attempted to put their own names forward as potential candidates. Among them, Zhang Junmai, Huang Yanpei, Luo Longji, and Tang Hualong were the most popular choices. However, in a surprising and controversial move, Zhang Lan has instead chosen Xian Ying, a fellow Sichuan native to be his PM. A longtime associate and friend of the President, Xian Ying's ascension has been interpreted by many as a sign of further consolidation of the CDL under its Sichuanese members.
Welcome Premier Xian!

Peering Under the Veil
The China Democratic League, though only formed shortly after the National Reconstruction Government's capture of Beijing, found its origins in the Federation of Chinese Democratic Parties. The FDCP, which was formed by various liberal, democratic, and progressive elements following Zhili victory against the Fengtian Clique, was organised to form a democratic political opposition to the Zhili Clique. The FDCP fractured following the 1932 League Uprising, when Cao Kun cancelled the 1933 elections. Many of the parties and societies which left the FDCP ended up in Southern China, and coalesced around the already-established Federalist Movement. Though composed of various conservative, liberal, and socialist elements, the CDL has found itself drifting to the left under Zhang Lan's leadership. Despite this, the CDL has largely remained intact thus far, due to Zhang Lan's inscrutable commitment to true democracy, and his steadfast opposition to all forms of authoritarianism. Zhang Lan's chairmanship has seen him champion the autonomy of parties partaking in the league, while affirming its goals of achieving genuine democracy.
The CDL welcomes all who dream of a Free China!
Little more than a collection of dreamers.
The Role of Liangism In the New China
During Emperor Guangxu's ambitious Hundred Days' Reform, above all other reformists stood Kang Youwei and Liang Qichao. Championing the ideas of a modern constitutional monarchy, Kang and Liang were forced to flee China when Empress Dowager Cixi alongside Yuan Shikai confined the Emperor to house arrest.\n\nWhile Kang remained a fervent royalist for the rest of his life, Liang came to the realisation that China's future lay with a republic, and espoused the German Bismarckian ideology of state socialism. During the Republic's history, Liang and his disciples emerged as some of the most influential thinkers, contesting Sun Yat-sen's more revolutionary brand of politics.\n\nLiang's political height came during the Anhui Clique's rule, where he and the Research Clique helped formulate the Anfu Party State's developmentalist policies and structure, before he fell out with Premier Duan Qirui. In the 20s, Liang drifted left, briefly becoming fascinated with reformist strands of socialism. One of Liang's final political moves was to establish the Chinese Constitutionalist Party, which was helmed by Xu Qin following his death.\n\nWhile the CCP tentatively cooperated with the Zhili Clique until their reorganisation into the DCP, Liang's disciple Zhang Junmai established the Democratic Socialist Party in Southern China in staunch opposition to Beijing. Although not directly politically descended from Liang, the Young China Party has claimed him as their spiritual leader as well. Although regarded as a divisive figure, few will shun his achievements to China's political development.
Liang's words shall continue to guide China into the future!
Liangism has run its course.
Zhang Junmai Contests Zhang Lan
Although Zhang Lan's Presidential campaign has been accepted by most within the China Democratic League at this point, there remain those who refuse to support him. Among them stands Zhang Junmai, leader of the Democratic Socialist Party. Although both men claim to follow strands of socialism, many have noted Zhang Junmai's more conservative stance and his assertive personality.\n\nAn egotistical man unsuited to working with others, Zhang Junmai has greatly resented the dominance of Sichuanese intellectuals within the League, regarding them as bit players who have only risen this far due to coincidence. Calling forth his party allies, as well as the other Liangist parties in the Democratic Constitutionalist Party and Young China Party, Zhang has declared his own Presidential Campaign.\n\nPulling from the same base, Zhang Junmai has attempted to sap the CDL support base away from Zhang Lan and the CDL, to the President's frustration. The infighting between the CDL and the Liangists has greatly crippled the CDL's ability to function. Capitalising upon this, Dan, Xiong and the Grand Republican Alliance have captured the initiative and now stand poised to dominate the electoral process, with many fearing that the GRA may go insofar as to rig the election itself, especially amongst Li Jiayu's Officer Department.
Zhang Junmai is the gift that keeps on giving.
Zhang Junmai with his pride and his ego!
Expressing China's Safeguard
With the China Democratic League in the midst of a civil war with Zhang Lan and Zhang Junmai fighting over leadership and the Grand Republican Alliance poised to win a blowout victory and rumours abound about their ambitions to end the fragile democracy in place, Liu Wenhui has asserted his prerogative as the largest and most powerful independent warlord within the National Reconstruction Government.\n\nLiu has called on the Grand Republican Alliance to end their pretensions of eroding federal democracy and the China Democratic League to keep its infighting from causing hyper partisanship. Liu has ominously warned that should this not occur, then he will force their hand to protect democratic institutions and the interests of the Chinese people. This has led to the public reacting worrisomely to the potential of a Liu Wenhui coup, while officials in government fear the sorts of careerists and opportunists who may ride Liu Wenhui's prospective coup to the top of Chinese government.
Zhang Lan intensifies his campaign against Zhang Junmai.
Xiong Kewu throws Liu Wenhui's letter in the trash.
Last Light of Chinese Democracy
For whatever reasons, be they that the Republic became inflamed with the hyper partisanship wrought by CDL infighting or that the Grand Republican Alliance overtly plotted to bring down Chinese democracy before it had a chance to live, it seems that neither the CDL nor the GRA will find themselves victorious.\n\nIn the past few hours, soldiers from the Baoding Department and other warlord armies seized strategic locations around the country and local polling stations. Meanwhile Liu Wenhui personally oversaw the seizure of President Zhang, the Cabinet, Xiong Kewu, and Dan Maoxin. In a nationwide announcement, Liu declared the overturning of the elections.\n\nLiu also formally announced his own bid for election in the interim, knowing that he will essentially run uncontested with all of his viable opponents under house arrest. Although a blatant power grab, few seem actually willing to oppose him, with many either fearing a new font of radicalism without Liu's intervention or find themselves too apathetic to care. Already, former Zhili officials have come out of semi-retirement to support Liu's bid, and it seems that the destroyed Beiyang order has re-asserted itself once more.
Pollice Compresso Favor Iudicabatur.
Hammer Striking the Anvil
For whatever reasons, be they that the Republic became inflamed with the passions of hyperpartisan squabbling, or that the Republic found itself engulfed in the waves of apathy, it seems that Chinese experiment with democracy has come to a sudden halt. In the past few hours, soldiers from the Baoding Department and other warlord armies seized strategic locations around the country and major governmental buildings in the capital. Minister of War Liu Wenhui, chief architect of this coup, personally oversaw the seizure of the President, his Cabinet, and the leading opposition.\n\nVery few figures have openly opposed Liu's coup, due to his careful politicking in the background and in his capacity with the cabinet. Managing to wrangle consensus with a few dissenters quickly shut down by group pressure, Liu has been swiftly sworn in as President. Surprisingly popular with the people, President Liu has managed to wrangle his own political machine as a form of \
Thus, Liu represents the voice of the people.
The Old Dispensation
Though he has always aimed to match the actions of the idealists around him, Liu Wenhui has more often than not found himself acting out of pragmatism and opportunism than he would like. Opportunism has always been a problem for China in Liu's eyes, and even as a new government is swept in, like so many before it, he cannot help but feel a slight disappointment. Did Yuan Shikai not try to seize power out of opportunism? Did the actions of scheming warlords not emerge from that same very place? Even in his own ranks, did his army not shrink and swell based on Liu's own fortunes?\n\nAs the Civil Governance and Progress Society rolls out its attempts at reform, Liu has watched helplessly as his colleagues each worked to stymie his more progressive policies, despite his greatest efforts. Back in Chengdu, thousands of miles away from the capital, reports and petitions have made their way onto Liu's desk, detailing Deng's speeches blatantly slamming the inefficiency of democracy, the staffing of old despotic lackeys into provincial posts and, of course, the nefarious machinations of Liu Wencai.\n\nNevertheless, Liu has worked tirelessly to address the concerns of his many critics and soothe tensions with an opposition frequently harassed by Liu's cronies, who utilise their new statuses to act with impunity and stifle the fledgling democratic apparatus. But perhaps this is merely a phenomenon seen in every administration's early days, all of which will settle like dust after the spring rain. What remains important is to observe the path ahead, to strive amidst hardship, to set ambitious goals and above all, to work diligently in all things. Liu may not be a perfect man, nor even an idealist like so many in the federalist movement, but his conviction remains that of a patriot, someone who keeps with the spirit of the November Riots and of China. Though China may never free itself of societal expediency, and those who act in self-interest will always plot in the shadows, pessimism will never be a solution.
A nation needs builders, not merely critics.
Democracy Eternally Remembered
The road towards federalism from Sichuan has certainly not been an easy one, constantly harassed and betrayed in the seemingly endless provincial struggle as local autocrats regularly outnumbered them. Frequently forced from their home province, Xiong and his men would only survive through the kindness of their republican brethren, though even that wasn't without its own disagreements. But look at how far they had come, the last flickering flame of a federalist dream that had not been stamped out, despite the time.\n\nNow the torch has been passed to Dan Maoxin, a new face on the political stage but a veteran of the republican struggle nonetheless, having been comrades with Xiong Kewu since the late 1910s. Owing to his legacy as a proponent for civil discussion and non-partisan unity, his dealings with independent republicans, the Political Science Clique and moderate members of the opposition have all earned him great praise as the \
Our ideals are something we must continue to struggle for.
Benevolence and Righteousness
Ever since the ailing days of the Qing and the fragmented republic that followed, many in the West have deemed China a backward nation that had been lost to the clutches of infighting and despotism. Certainly, in some respects, it has rung true, no place more than in Sichuan, itself torn apart by local warlords for decades. Through such strife, Zhang Lan has witnessed it all, the emaciated peasants crushed under taxes, the few businessmen leaving the province for more prosperous opportunities in the East and, of course, the various military men who fought each other back and forth across the Red Basin.\n\nHaving navigated the political landscape of China and personally led the modern federalist movement to national prominence, Zhang has realised the need not only for Western ideas; that of democracy for all, freedom of thought and intellectual independence, but also the need for virtues championed by Chinese philosophers many millennia ago. First is the idea of benevolence, which Zhang believed many of the republic's previous leaders lacked, despite being cunning, smart and courageous, they neither won the hearts of the people nor worked in the interest of the masses. But of course, benevolence alone has often been misguided in China, the stumbling bureaucracy of Beiyang, the revolutionary zealotry of the KMT and the populism of warlords alike all claimed to be for the grandiose goal of National Salvation, but failed to act steadily, justly and fairly enough to win the people over. The Chinese Democratic League, however, is the culmination of both these ideas, standing on the side of the nation, striving for unity, both saving the country and building it anew as it marches on into the new century.\n\nOf course, even as the guns fall silent across China and the people ready themselves for the fruits of democracy to come, Zhang and his colleagues remain ever vigilant of the various forces that seek to rip away the agency of the masses. For Zhang, this enthusiasm must continue to be utilised, harnessed and nurtured from the masses, because what is reform, if not a steady revolution of its own?
We shall persevere unrelentingly! We shall never yield!

Liu Bocheng
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 4th, 1892, in Kaixian County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §SPAC§!\n§YBackground:§! §LWorking on the farm in his teenage years due to his father's death, Liu joined the Sichuan Military Government Army Officer School in 1912. He served under Xiong Kewu after graduation. Liu took part in the Second Revolution of 1913 against Yuan Shikai when Xiong declared his opposition to Beijing. However, Yuan would emerge victorious, and Liu went into exile in Shanghai. While there, Liu joined Sun Yat-sen's Revolutionary Party, the precursor to the Kuomintang.\n\nLiu returned to Sichuan to organise a National Protection Army when Cai E marched against Yuan Shikai's imperial gambit in 1915. During the war in 1916, Liu was shot twice in the head, leaving his right eye blind. In 1924, Liu engaged against the Zhili clique forces of Wu Peifu, and won the battle of Longquanyi. It was in 1924 that Liu would become influenced by the tenets of Marxism and the goals of the National Revolution. In 1926, Liu was formally folded into the Kuomintang.\n\nWhile Liu supported the Guangzhou Nationalist Government in its attempt to take Wuhan by preventing the local Sichuanese warlords from aiding Wu Peifu, the KMT not only failed to take Wuhan but collapsed entirely. Liu's sudden isolation from his allies caused the other Sichuanese warlords to push Liu to the edges of Sichuan, where he remains in hiding to this day. Liu hopes that one day, the Kuomintang will return to the Mainland and the peasants will be free from reactionary oppression.§!
Zhang Lan
Son of a scholar and schoolteacher, Zhang Lan would study with his father at a young age, becoming a scholar himself in 1894, and entering the Zunjing Academy in Chengdu in 1902. Selected to study in Japan in 1903, he would enter Tokyo Hongwen Academy, returning to China after Anti-Cixi remarks. Serving in various teaching roles, he was elected to the Sichuan Advisory Council in 1909 but refused to accept the position, instead opting to serve in the reformist constitutionalist movement in the twilight years of the Qing.\n\nParticipating in the Sichuan Railway Protection Movement, he would become immersed in the Xinhai foray of Sichuan, before being elected as a Member of the Assembly in 1913. In 1915 he would participate in the National Protection War, before becoming Beijing governor of Sichuan in 1917; fleeing from Chengdu with Liu Cunhou in 1918, and later returning to Beijing. In 1919 he would support the New Culture Movement, and aid in sending young students to the radical intellectual scene of Europe.\n\nIn 1920 he would return to Nanchong, devoting himself to education, and in 1925 even became president of Chengdu University. Advocating freedom of thought and academic freedom, he enabled the various functions of local revolutionary groups under his auspices. Resigning in 1932 over the Zhili Suppression of the Shanghai Uprising, he would return to Nanchong to work on education matters. Participating in the formation of the FCDP that same year, Zhang would garner magnitudes of respect for his devotion to democracy, and role as a mediating hand.

Xian Ying
§YBorn:§! §L1885, in Xichong County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §SNCRA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBorn into poverty, Xian Ying needed the support of is relatives to attend early private schooling. A young student of Zhang Lan, Xian would become his special protégé, as a mentor is to a student, and as friends. In 1908, Xiang Ying enrolled in the Sichuan Accelerated School, joining the military. Simultaneously, he joined the Tongmenghui, and became swift friends with He Guoguang, Pan Wenhua, Yang Sen, and Liu Xiang.\n\nFollowing Zhang Lan around Sichuan, and unto the House of Representatives in Beijing, Xian would resign with his mentor in 1914 and attend the Army University as pretext. Travelling South to join the National Protection Movement, he would remain close with Zhang, starting a Pro-November Riots Newspaper on his behalf that flirted with socialist ideas. In 1921, Xian would participate in the Sichuan Warlord fray, helping Liu Xiang extensively.\n\nFirst flirting with industry since the 1920s, Xian's passion for military service would transition to industrial pursuits, using his connections with Zhang Lan to travel around the country, both for business, and to meet likeminded men such as Liang Shuming, and Yan Yangchu. Serving under He Guoguang in 1935, Xian would be Inspector General of Nanchong, but due to a scandal with his secretary, Gu Xinghua, Xian would be forced out of his position, instead serving as manager of Zhang Lan's Jianhua Middle School in Nanchong. Closely connected with his mentor, Xian has slowly become a proponent of Third Force Chinese Democratic politics.§!
Che Yaoxian
§YBorn:§! §LSeptember 27th, 1894, in Dayi County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §SNCRA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LLiving an early life of destitution, Che Yaoxian would enter a private school at the age of five, albeit after a family lawsuit would be forced to drop out. Selling produce to support his family, he would become a merchant's apprentice in 1908, and begin a lifelong interest in reading; by 1911, he was an accountant at 17. In 1912, the young Che joined the army, serving in the Sichuan Army, and by 1918 had become a company commander, rising to a regimental commander in Liu Xiang's army.\n\nA representative to the Kuomintang, Che would attempt to coax Liu Xiang into defection, but after Liu rebuked his efforts, Che resigned from the army and travlled to Japan to study. With the Kuomintang's defeat, Che turned his hopes towards the various other left-wing movements within the nation; most important of which being the Left-FCDP. In 1928, Che returned to Chengdu and subsequently joined the KMT in secret. Utilising his connections, he joined Liu Wenhui's Army.\n\nRallying local Christian groups in Chengdu to support the Eastern Sichuan Insurgents, Che would initiate an onslaught of patriotic Christian socialist organisations in Chengdu, and after the Shanghai Uprising, denounced Yang Sen's Government and Beijing. Almost arrested by Xiang Chuanyi, Che would cooperate with Leng Yindong and subsequently serve under Liu Wenhui and Deng Xihou's Baoding armies. Continuing to remain in contact with local oppositional groups, Che hs taken a special interest in Zhang Lan's efforts within the basin.§!
Zhou Taixuan
§YBorn:§! §LFebruary 14th, 1895, in Xindu County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §SNCRA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LProgressing through a standard education, Zhou would graduate from Middle School in 1911, and in 1913 would follow Wei Siluan, Hu Xuan, and others to the Political Economy Department of the China Public School. After graduating in 1916, Zhou worked as a translator for the Shanghai Minxin Daily, and later for the Beijing-based Jinghua Daily and Zhonghua Xinbao, as well as Zhonghua News Agency.\n\nIn 1918 he introduced Wang Guangqi to Jinghua Daily, and through Wang met Li Dazhao. Swiftly growing close, Zhou and Li decided to establish the Young China Society in 1919 with Wang's help. Sailing for France, he would be admitted to the University of Montpellier, and established the Paris News Agency to inform China of events in France.\n\nObtaining a Master's Degree in Education, Zhou entered the University of Paris in 1924, remaining in France from 1924 to 1928. In 1930 he declined to stay at the University, and returned to China at the invitation of Zhang Lan; serving at Zhang Lan's educational institutions in Sichuan. As recent as 1936, Zhou has reportedly wrote a series of articles lambasting the Capitalistic order in Europe, and is one of the founding members of the New Century Society.§!
Liu Siying
§YBorn:§! §LFebruary 24th, 1895, in Nanchuan, Chongqing, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §nYCP§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBorn during the ultimate decade of the 19th century, Liu Siying like many of his fellow countrymen embraced the underlying current of nationalism of the time. Seeking a modern education, Liu attended Hosei University in Japan. After graduating, he found work in Shanghai as editor of Shanghai Jiguo Times. During the 20s, Liu would join the newly formed Young China Party and play a crucial role in its operations. On the orders of the Party leadership, Liu returned to Sichuan to work for the Zhili warlord Yang Sen as his secretary.\n\nThrough Yang, Liu was able to establish a working relationship with Wu Peifu, who sought the support of a modern mass party to stand against the Kuomintang during the Northern Expedition. Through Liu, party leader Zeng Qi was able to greatly expand his influence across Northern China. Under the supervision of Li Huang, the local YCP leader of Sichuan, Liu has worked to spread the party message across all of Sichuan both through his military contacts and through his capacity as the Chengdu Southwest Public School. Thanks to his efforts working with local gentry to support ailing refugees, the YCP enjoys extensive support across all levels of Sichuanese society.§!\n\n§YNotes: Though a close ally of the Zhili Clique, Liu serves only the interests of the YCP first and foremost, in order to bring about China's long awaited Plebiscitarian Revolution.§!
Xiong Kewu
Born into a family of doctors, ever since he was young, Xiong Kewu held a love of learning. He became intrigued by the ideas of democracy and modernism presented by the West and lamented that China had fallen gravely behind the rest of the world. In 1903, he attended the Sichuan East Literature Hall and later became acquainted with revolutionaries such as Dai Jitao. After achieving the sponsorship of his uncle, Xiong went to study in Japan, where he met Sun Yat-sen and would later join Sun's Tongmenghui. After the Xinhai Revolution, Xiong served as commander of the Dahan Sichuan Military Government until 1913. Yuan Shikai and the Beiyang Government expelled Sun's forces from China, and Xiong was forced to flee to Japan in exile.\n\nYet, the defeat did not deter Xiong from dreaming of a free China. In Japan and Southeast Asia, he raised funds for the revolution and returned to China to join Cai E in the victorious National Protection War against Yuan. Despite being comrades in the National Protection War, Xiong turned against his former ally Lü Chao in 1920 and successfully expelled him from the province with the help of the warlord Liu Cunhou. In that same year, he issued a firm declaration that the people of Sichuan would no longer play second fiddle to the Beiyang and Guangzhou Governments that had arisen in the chaos of the Warlord Period. This did not last long as Sichuan collapsed into civil war and Liu Cunhou joined forces with the Beiyang Government, and Xiong was once again forced to flee into exile. He then fled to Guangdong, joining the Chinese Kuomintang, and participated in its first Congress in 1924.\n\nXiong's brief stay in the Kuomintang ended in 1925 when he was betrayed by Wang Jingwei and Chiang Kai-shek on charges that he was colluding with the Chinese Federalist leader Chen Jiongming. After the Northern Expedition's failure and the Kuomintang's breakdown, Xiong returned home to Sichuan dissatisfied with political life. And yet his passion for republican ideals never died, and although he has not forgotten the betrayal of the KMT, he lends an open ear to the Chinese Federalists in Guangdong.
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 26th, 1885, in Jingyan County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YBackground:§! §L$SZC_xiong_kewu_bio$\n\nWith his success in the recent civil war in Sichuan, Xiong will ensure that Sichuan is turned into an impenetrable fortress of republicanism and one that will help end years of authoritarianism in Beijing.§!
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 26th, 1885, in Jingyan County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §fUFAA§!\n§YBackground:§! §L$SZC_xiong_kewu_bio$§!
Dan Maoxin
Determined to devote his life to the pursuit of revolution and truth, Dan Maoxin would join the Tongmenghui in 1905 at the behest of Sun Yat-sen and Huang Xing while studying in Japan. Returning to China in 1906, Dan would serve as a propaganda chief and liaison officer while simultaneously establishing a public school in Shanghai to spread democratic ideals. Captured for his actions at the Second Guangzhou Uprising, but being released, Dan would return to Sichuan and serve a provincial post before resigning due to Sichuan Governor Hu Jingyi's autocratic rule.\n\nIt would be in Chongqing where Dan would become Xiong's chief of staff, his experience throughout the numerous revolutions hardening his resolve and convincing him of the need for revolutionary unity. As such, Dan would also uniquely be one of the few who avidly sought to work with Yunnan and Guizhou, citing their shared respect for Southern republican ideals. Regardless of policy, Dan would loyally command troops against Sichuan's Zhili-aligned hardliners like Yang Sen and Liu Cunhou even as the tide gradually turned against the federalists.\n\nIn Tongren, Dan remains Xiong's second in command and a prominent voice of unity within the movement, open to cooperation between aligned democratic movements and provinces.

Yu Peidi
§YBorn:§! §L1889, in Zizhong County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §fUFAA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGoing to Japan to study with his brother Yu Peilun in 1905, Yu Peidi would study at a military school in Yokohama before returning to China in 1908 to try and join the Hekou Uprising, though finding out about its failure before he made it back to China. Travelling around China for a period, Yu would spend the next few years drifting between Japan and Guangzhou before hearing of the martyrdom of his own brother in the Second Guangzhou Uprising.\n\nReturning to his hometown of Neijiang following this tragedy, Yu would actively participate in the Railway Protection Movement and the formation of the ensuing republican governments in Sichuan, joining Xiong Kewu's ranks in the process. Yu continued to show his command capabilities during his time as a subordinate to Dan Maoxin, following the Nine Man Regiment to Guangzhou after their expulsion from Sichuan, even as lesser officers and common soldiers returned to their homes. Hearing of Xiong Kewu's detention and being stripped of his military command by the KMT, Yu would spend the intervening years in Neijiang, occasionally making trips to Japan and the Legations. Now joining Xiong's recouped divisions in Tongren, Yu remains a reliable albeit slightly inexperienced commander, a close confidant of Xiong and a prominent communicator with the local intelligentsia.§!\n\n§YNotes: Over the past few years, Yu has travelled to Japan and Moscow on many occasions with Xiong Kewu to meet with Hu Hanmin, remaining a good personal friend of the statesman.§!

Xiao Yisu
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 11th, 1899, in Peng'an County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §fUFAA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBeing expelled from school at a young age for engaging in revolutionary activity, Xiao Yisu would study at the prestigious Yunnan Military Academy with financial assistance from his aunt. Originally lacking any kind of intense political persuasion, Xiao would serve in the ranks of Yang Sen's army as a battalion commander before defecting to General Tang Zimo, an individual aligned more with the Sichuanese KMT.\n\nAfter Tang's unexpected death in a party purge orchestrated by Chiang Kai-shek in 1925, Xiao would be relieved of his post and grow slightly disillusioned with the KMT, finding employment in the ranks of Guo Rudong's army. Here, Xiao would spend the latter half of the 1920s commanding troops under Guo while still being receptive to more republican ideals. Chafing under Guo's more arbitrary command and being dismissed in 1932, Xiao would become listless, spending time in the Legation Cities. His second wind would come later that year, when the Shanghai Uprising would send a lot of China's Republican mouthpieces fleeing West for fear of being killed or suppressed within the League. Xiao fled to Hunan, not wanting to be caught in the chaos, connecting with various intellectuals there, joining Xiong Kewu when he heard that his military expertise could once again be of some use. In Xiong's officer corps, he has made a name for himself by being hard-working and diligently studying army command, showing promise in the many battles that lie ahead for the federalist cause.§!
Bian Zhishan
§YBorn:§! §L1899, in Chongqing, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §fUFAA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LOriginally destined for a career in academics and the arts, being recommended by his father's friend Yang Shukan to study in Nanjing Zhongyi Academy, then abroad, Bian instead enrolled in the Baoding Army Officers' School. Graduating in 1920, he would join Xiong Kewu's army as a deputy regimental commander before being promoted. Commanding troops under Xiong, Bian would fight with conviction throughout the 1920s, even following Xiong into Hunan and then Guangdong, commanding a minor officer cadre school during this time.\n\nAfter Xiong Kewu's detention at Humen Fortress by the KMT in 1925, Bian would be relieved of his post and become chief of the infantry department at the Zhu Peide Military Academy, a subsidiary of Whampoa. Associated with the Whampoa school affairs committee until Xiong's pardoning by Hu Hanmin, Bian would rejoin his former superior as adjutant general of Xiong's military police. A relatively young general in Sichuan's federalist cause, Bian is completely open to collaboration with other republican movements and even the more radical elements of both KMT and CSP. During his time spent around Tongren, Bian has also become proficient in leading and raising civilian levies and militias on top of his regular infantry, which proves useful in rooting out bandits and navigating the fluid battlefields of Sichuan.§!\n\n§YNotes: On top of his regular duties, Bian has also acted as Xiong's liaison and chief of both the security guard corps and civilian labour unit in Tongren.§!
Li Weiru
§YBorn:§! §L1883, in Fuling, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §fUFAA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LStudying in Japan in 1904, Li Weiru would join the Tongmenghui in 1906, being assigned by Sun Yat-sen to return to Sichuan in 1907 to aid in Tongmenghui efforts there. Conspiring with Xiong Kewu, Gao Yaheng, and others, he aided in the development of revolutionary cells, and in the initial Xinhai solidarity revolution within Sichuan. In a period spanning over a decade from 1911 to 1923, he served as deputy commander of the Fuling Military Government, Chief of Staff of the Shu Army, Chief of Staff of the Governor, and director of Sichuan Military Academy.\n\nIn 1924, he retired from the army, returning to farming. In 1927, Li had served in various local positions raising funds to establish progressive schools. Retaining close contact with Liu Bocheng and others, Li would eventually flirt with the concepts of socialism as prescribed by the Left Kuomintang. Creating a peasant army from 1926 onwards, Li would throw himself into Sichuan's political foray, combatting men like Liu Xiang.\n\nNarrowly avoiding death, Li would work together with Liu, and eventually Xiong Kewu's progressive Nine-Man Regiment, to wage a guerrilla campaign against the Beiyang warlords occupying Sichuan. Winning the hearts and minds of the peasantry, Li would prove to be a vital lifeline for the guerrilla's continued success.§!
Liu Guanglie
§YBorn:§! §L1889, in Renshou, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §fUFAA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LParticipating in the Xinhai Revolution in China, Liu Guanglie would serve as Director of the Sichuan Provincial Government, and the Southern Representative in the 1919 North-South Peace Talks. Later, Liu would serve in various capacities as commissioner of Nanchong, and Mianyang, before studying Buddhism in Japan in 1924.\n\nReturning with the support of other Buddhists, Liu would found the Tibetan Language Institute at Ciyin Temple in Beijing, inviting Dorje Jueba Geshe to be an instructor teaching Tibetan language, and Tibetan Buddhism.\n\nHaving returned to his home province following the 1932 Shanghai Uprising, Liu would tacitly link himself towards the progressive resistance efforts of Xiong Kewu and his Nine-Man Regiment, working in a limited capacity as a sympathetic local official.§!
Shi Tiyuan
§YBorn:§! §L1890, in Xuanhan County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §fUFAA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LIntelligent from his youth, Shi Tiyuan would excel in his studies, becoming a scholar at age 14. In 1911 he graduated from Sichuan Higher Police School, and would participate in the Sichuan Railway Protection Movement that same year. Launching an Anti-Qing uprising, he would establish a local county military government, and would become a member of the new revolutionary Provisional Provincial Government in 1912.\n\nIn 1914 he became a Chinese teacher at a local school, before becoming chief director of a Chongqing Salt Transport company, and President of Chongqing Commercial Daily. From 1916 he served in the Huguojun as a staff officer in various capacities, before returning to his teaching job in 1918. In 1922 he served as secretary to Deng Xihou, and Secretary-General and Chief Administrator of the Chongqing Garrison, and worked within the provincial finance department.\n\nIn 1927 Shi became Secretary General to Yang Sen's 3rd Division, and from 1928 was secretary to Pan Wenhua, and later to the Chongqing Municipal Government, Eduaction Bureau, and Public Security Bureau. In 1932 he became director of the Chongqing Commodity Inspection Office, and in 1933 was general manager of Chongqing Electric Power. From 1934 until now, Shi has served as mayor of Chongqing, working to promulgate various progressive reforms within the expanding city.§!
Hu Ziang

Actively participating as a young student, he would later enter Beijing Agricultural College in 1919, and would maintain his revolutionary streak by participating in the November Riots. After graduation in 1923, he returned to Chongqing to serve as a teacher and principle at his old middle school, president of the county Farmers' Association, and Director of the Chongqing Education Bureau.\n\nJoining the military in 1926, he would serve in Liu Wenhui's army, before becoming director of the Border Affairs Office of the Chengdu Sichuan Border Defence Supervisory Office. Aiding in the developing of farms and banks, alongside the banning of opium and reduction of taxes, he would briefly moonlight in Jiangxi as a member of the Agricultural College, before returning to Chongqing in 1933.\n\nCo-founding the Chongqing Huaxi Industrial Company, he would oversee various industrial endeavours all-throughout Sichuan, becoming something of a renowned industrial magnate in Sichuan. Xiong Zhefan
§YBorn:§! §L1894, Yanjingwan , Jingyan County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §fUFAA§!\n§YBackground:§! §LThe younger brother of Xiong Kewu, Xiong Zhefan studied in France in his early years during the country's greatest period of national strife. Following his brother closely in his endeavours, Xiong Zhefan's true talents would arise in his capacity to manage administrative affairs on behalf of his brother. Li Jiayu
A career soldier, Li Jiayu would graduate from the fourth phase of the Sichuan Army Primary School just in time to participate in a student army during the Xinhai Revolution. Post-revolution, he would enlist in the first phase of the Sichuan Army Officer Academy, and in 1913, would transfer to the Nanjing Army Officers' Prepatory School. In the summer of the same year, Li Jiayu would see service in the first of many revolts against Yuan Shikai, ultimately failing and returning to Sichuan.\n\nEntering the third phase of the Army Officer Academy, Li Jiayu would find himself within the 4th Division of Sichuan Army under Liu Cunhou, later becoming a brigade commander in Deng Xihou's 3rd Division. Ultimately being promoted to Army Major General in 1923, Li would become commander of the First Division, and in 1926 would break off from Deng Xihou's nominal Baoding Department to assist in the formation of the Officer's Department.\n\nDuring their tenure in exile, Li Jiayu managed to keep the torch of revolutionary republicanism alive within the hearts of many of his fellow cadres, and is intent on maintaining that course.

Luo Zezhou

Steadily rising through the ranks and acting as Chongqing Police Chief in 1923, Luo would capitalise on his successes against Yang Sen in 1925 to advance into Chengdu and seize its key offices, being appointed commander of the 11th Division in August of the same year.\n\nSpending the latter half of the 1920s stationed in Suining, Luo would join Li Jiayu in forming the Qunyi Society, aiming to attract alumni from the Officers School into their ranks. Luo would see action once more during the Central Sichuan War of 1929, mutinying against his former superior Deng Xihou and leading his troops against Liu Xiang, ultimately emerging from the conflict with a mixed military record but a firm hold over Nanchong. Lai Xinhui
§YBorn:§! §LAugust 1884, Santai County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §lOfficer Department§!\n§YBackground:§! §LServing as a regimental trainee after he graduated from Yunnan Army Academy in 1911, Lai Xinhui would participate in Yunnan's Double Ninth Uprising before finding himself in the ranks of the Sichuan army as a commander of an artillery battalion. Fighting in Liu Cunhou's army throughout the late 1910s, Lai would rise steadily and be provided with new artillery pieces from Hanyang arsenals, earning prestige for coming to his superior's aid many times in battles against both Xiong Kewu and other republican forces.\n\nThe invasion of Sichuan by forces from Yunnan and Guizhou throughout the 1920s would see Lai Xinhui come under the command of various Sichuan militarists. However, he would become acquainted with Liu Chengxun in 1922 and remain a close colleague throughout the following years. Lai's voice of support for the Zhili government in 1924 would pay dividends, as he would be appointed commander of Sichuan's 1st Division and then governor of Sichuan in 1925, though Yang Sen would quickly supplant him.\n\nReturning to service under Liu Chengxun, who now controlled areas in Southern Xikang, Lai would spend his time eliminating bandits both in the local area and his own ranks. The 1929 Central Sichuan war would spell bad news for Lai and Liu, as both men would be routed from Xikang, with Liu retiring to his hometown of Dayi and Lai joining the ranks of the Officer Department, bringing with him his men and decades of experience.§!
Chen Lanting
§YBorn:§! §L1887, Shizhu County Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §lOfficer Department§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAn ardent student of the Green Forest School and coming from a life of brigandage, Chen would enter the military by leading a band of his men under the command of Liu Bocheng in 1916. Following this streak of republicanism and joining Xiong Kewu's army in 1920, Chen Lanting would be promoted to brigade commander in 1923 before surrendering to Lai Xinhui's forces in 1924 and becoming his subordinate.\n\nChen's tenure stationed in Luzhou as part of Lai Xinhui's 3rd Brigade was uneventful for the most part, but did result in major tensions with his colleagues, such as Li Zhangfu. It would be this tension that allowed Chen to be approached by socialist guerrillas in 1926, opportunistically throwing his lot in with the Lushun uprising. The failure of both the Lushun uprising and Northern Expedition would see Chen defect back and forth between the province's strongmen, switching from the ranks of Liu Xiang to Yang Sen, then back to Lai Xinhui, where he'd stay throughout the 1930s.\n\nLeading his troops during the chaotic Central Sichuan War, Chen would be a relatively minor player, primarily seeing to the defence of Neijiang and not seeing much action otherwise.§!\n\n§YNotes: Holding onto notable connections with Sichuan's brotherhood sects - the Paoge, Chen remains a sworn brother with the likes of Fan Shaozeng, as well as smugglers and gentry alike.§!
Li Zongfang
§YBorn:§! §L1891, Mengyang, Peng County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §lOfficer Department§!\n§YBackground:§! §LIn 1909 Li Zongfang enrolled in the fourth class of the Sichuan Army Primary School, and after Xinhai, was transferred to the Sichuan Army Officers School. In 1923 he became a brigade commander, and by 1927 he had become a brigade commander under Li Jiayu, steadily rising in rank and position.§!
Chen Li
§YBorn:§! §LMay 1892, Anyue County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §lOfficer Department§!\n§YBackground:§! §LEnlisting in the ranks of Chongqing's student army in the opening days of Xinhai, Chen Li would enrol into the Artillery department of the Sichuan Army Officer School, graduating in 1915. He would be frequently promoted during his tenure as an intern under Li Jiayu's command in Liu Cunhou's army, commanding his own brigade by 1925. Being stationed in Sichuan's basin counties, Chen would serve as a pillar of stability in a region which had been stricken with poverty, famine and lawlessness owing to its proximity to Chengdu.\n\nAs the years progressed, Chen would unknowingly induct into his brigade many members of the CSP, who exploited Chen's more neutral stance towards socialism as an opportunity to recruit for their own cause. This would culminate in the Guanghan Mutiny, carried out by the West Sichuan branch of the CSP in tandem with the Gujunba Uprising, whereby the common soldiers of Chen Li's army rose up against their officers, intending to join their compatriots in the east. Fortunately for Chen, his quick return to his garrison area (along with the dispersal of the rebels by Tian Songyao's forces) would restore order to his brigade.\n\nDeng's forgiveness of Chen for his mistake would soothe tensions between the two men, resulting in Chen's ambivalence to the rebellion of his colleagues Li Jiayu and Luo Zezhou in 1929. Privy to both the interests of the Baoding and Officers, Chen walks a line of neutrality, willing to throw his lot in with whoever comes out on top.§!
Kuang Jixun

Following his superior through thick and thin, Kuang's inherent progressive sympathies would shine with his membership of the Young Soldier's Federation. Tacitly working together with men such as Liu Bocheng in their guerrilla operations, Kuang enabled a semi-legal avenue for the socialist guerrillas to receive aid and materiel from Sichuan Army stockpiles.§!
Peng Shifu

Zhou Conghua
§YBorn:§! §L1896, in Xinfan, Chengdu, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §lOfficer Department§! - §SYoung Soldier's Federation§!\n§YBackground:§! §LDropping out of school from a young age, Zhou Conghua would join the army, and in 1917 he rose to the rank of platoon leader, and enrolled in the Officer Training School sponsored by Liu Xiang. After graduation, Liu served as a company commander under Liu Yujiu in the Sichuan Army, before being transferred to Liu Xiang upon Yujiu's defeat.\n\nEnrolled into Baoding Military Academy by Liu, in-part to maintain a check on Zhili intrigues, Zhou's diligence, active thinking, bravery, and strategic prowess gained the attention of Tang Zaili. Utilising his relationship with Tang, Zhou would relay all information relevant to Sichuan matters towards Liu Xiang. Lü Chao
A native of Sichuan, Lü graduated in 1909 from the Sichuan Army Primary School. He then joined the Fourth Nanjing Army Secondary School, which he then abandoned to join Sun Yat-sen's revolutionary Tongmenghui to resist and fight for a free Republican China. Like many others from his province, in 1910, he attended the Baoding Academy, which he then left when the Xinhai Revolution broke out in 1911. He then travelled to Shanghai, where he worked with Chen Qimei, Wang Jingwei, and Li Shizeng (prominent associates of Dr. Sun) until he was forced to flee to Sichuan in exile after Yuan Shikai took power.\n\nIn 1915, the National Protection War to resist Yuan's regime in Beijing broke out, and Lü joined fellow Sichuanese Xiong Kiwu as part of Cai E's army in Sichuan. When Chen Jiongming and the Federalist movement attempted to kick Sun Yat-sen out of Guangdong, Lü took the side of Sun and fought against his former comrade Xiong Kiwu for control of Sichuan. He then joined Sun in exile in Shanghai and returned with Sun in his return to Guangdong in 1923 and became one of Sun's trusted allies in the Kuomintang. During the doomed Northern Expedition, Lü represented the Sichuanese forces, and after the mission's failure, he fled back to his native Sichuan in exile once more. Yet, despite numerous defeats, the revolutionary spirit installed within Lü never died out. Instead, he continued to bide his time, believing fervently in the principles illustrated by the late eternal premier of the Kuomintang. As he waited in exile under the Yang Sen regime that once again took control of Sichuan, he maintained his contacts with the Kuomintang throughout the country.

Deng Xihou
§YBorn:§! §LMay 24th, 1889, in Yingshan County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YBackground:§! §LDeng joined the army in 1906 and went on to enrol in the Baoding Academy, though he would later discontinue his studies to return to Sichuan when the Xinhai Revolution occurred. Deng went on to rise through the ranks over the years until he was promoted to general in 1923. Having been more active behind a desk than on the various battlefields during this turbulent time in China earned him a reputation as a trustworthy and competent careerist, though he hardly earned much fame and fortune in the process. While he was respected among his colleagues from the Baoding Department, he would eventually go on to be subordinated to Yang Sen as the Zhili Clique favoured the Armament Department over the Baoding Department.\n\nDespite not having the favour of the Zhili, the Baoding Department managed to cooperate with the Armament Department, putting them in a position where they were able to fill the vacuum in Sichuan left by the eventual collapse of Yang Sen's clique in the Sichuanese Civil War of 1936. As the most prominent figure in the more traditionalist side of the Baoding Clique, his reforms focus on rural development and improving the lot of the farmers of Sichuan. His widespread support in the countryside and the army combined with the broader support earned by his diarchy partner Liu Wenhui has put the Baoding Department in a position of political flexibility rarely seen among China's warlord cliques. He aims to turn Sichuan into a model province for the rest of China, generally putting the well-being of Sichuan's people ahead of ideology or lofty expansionist ambitions.§!
§YBorn:§! §LMay 24th, 1889, in Yingshan County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §cBaoding Department§! - §cDeng Faction§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAfter joining the army in 1906 Deng was admitted to the Sichuanese Military Academy. After graduating in 1909 he enrolled into the Baoding Academy. After the Xinhai Revolution, Deng discontinued his studies and returned to Sichuan to continue his military service there instead. After the Republic of China was established Deng joined the Sichuan Fourth Division under Liu Cunhou. Deng successively held the post of adjutant and went on to rise through the ranks to become a battalion commander by 1916, the commander of the Fifth Brigade in 1917 and an independent brigade commander by February 1918. From 1920 to 1923 Deng participated in various battles in Yunnan, Guizhou and Sichuan and performed well enough to become the commander of the Third Division. In December of the same year, the Beijing government established by the Zhili clique had him promoted to the rank of general. He would maintain this rank after being subordinated to Yang Sen when he was made the military governor of Sichuan.\n\nDeng is a careerist through-and-through, with a service record that had more stationings, transfers and promotions than actual battles by the time he became the most prominent figure among Sichuan's Baoding graduates. While no one disputes that he's competent at what he does, his experience in the past has been far more theoretical than practical compared to his more battle-hardened peers.§!
Tian Songyao
In 1912, Tian joined the Sichuan Army under Liu Cunhou's 4th division. Although a member of the Tongmenghui during the Qing years, Tian aligned with Yuan Shikai during the Second Revolution of 1913. Liu Cunhou ultimately aligned against Yuan in the National Protection War a few years later, and Tian served as a captain. In 1917, Liu launched a campaign to retake Chengdu, and successfully took Chongqing and ousted the Yunnan and Guizhou forces in Sichuan.\n\nIn December, Tang Jiyao reignited the war and pushed Liu out of Chengdu. Beijing reorganised Liu's army into the 21st division and Tian was made Major General. By the end of the year, Liu put Tian in control of the 21st division. Liu made an alliance with Liu Xiang, and together they pushed Xiong Kewu out of Sichuan. In 1920, Xiong and Liu made an alliance against Yunnan, and they pushed Yunnanese forces out of Sichuan. The following year, Xiong allied with Liu Xiang and they pushed Liu out of Sichuan.\n\nTian's 21st division was folded into the new Sichuanese power structure. In 1922, Liu Xiang was defeated by Liu Chengxun and he took control of most of Liu Xiang's former domain. Deng Xihou of the Baoding Department was unhappy with the division of the spoils, and convinced fellow Baoding graduate Tian to join him against Liu, which saw the coalition win. When Liu Cunhou returned to Sichuan as Yang's subordinate, Tian realigned with his former commander, but maintains ties with the other warlords in case.

Sun Zhen
§YBorn:§! §LFebruary 5th 1892, in Mianzhu, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §cBaoding Department§! - §aTian Faction§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBrought up with a solid education but deeply changed by the death of his father and sister at a young age, Sun Zhen would abandon his future as a scholar to become a soldier, joining the Baoding Army Academy in 1912.\n\nSun, along with his classmates, would actively participate in both the Railway Protection Movement and the ensuing uprisings in Wuchang and the Eastern Seaboard. However, Sun would not return to his studies after the failed Second Revolution, returning to Chongqing to prepare for another uprising that would never come to fruition.\n\nAfter the tragedy of his mother's death in 1914, Sun would fall into unemployment before being offered a position as a platoon leader under Tian Songyao by one of his friends. Sun would go on to distinguish himself during the National Protection War by personally leading a 40-man assault squad to capture the town of Naxi, resulting in his promotion to the commander of a cavalry regiment.\n\nHe would continue to serve under Tian Songyao as part of Liu Cunhou's Jingchuan Army, now fighting against Xiong Kewu's forces. It would be here where he would command a daring 3-week campaign against his foes, employing everything from surprise attacks to tactical bluffs.\n\nGrowing his prestige during the 20s and 30s, Sun would continue to be one of Tian Songyao's most reliable commanders, being dispatched by Tian to aid Liu Cunhou against the Eastern Sichuan guerrillas and honing his proficiency in Sichuan's more difficult terrain.§!

Liu Wenhui
§YBorn:§! §LJanuary 10th, 1895, in Dayi County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YBackground:§! §LA lifelong military man, Liu Wenhui has been in the army since he was thirteen. He enrolled into the Sichuanese Military Academy in 1911, and subseequently enrolled into the Baoding Army Academy in 1914, and went on to graduate from it in 1916. While he owes a lot of his success and influence through nepotism and convenient connections, he's nonetheless managed to become a respected and influential figure within the clique. After the end of the Northern Expedition, Wenhui went on to side fully with Deng Xihou and would go on to become one of the clique's more notorious members.\n\nAfter the defeat of his nephew Liu Xiang in the Sichuanese Civil War, the Baoding department ascended to power. His friendship with Deng Xihou earned him a position of leadership with what has become the country's diarchy where he - in stark contrast to his nephew - represents a more reformist branch within the diarchy. Popular within the military and the more urban administrative class, he's allowed the department to broaden its support far beyond the conservative constituents that Xihou appeals to. While he's unlikely to make it into the history books as one of China's greatest military leaders, as long as the cooperation between the two friends in the diarchies continues he's sure to be remembered for his stalwart efforts to rapidly modernise Sichuan and improve the lot of the province's citizens.§!
§YBorn:§! §LJanuary 10th, 1895, in Dayi County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §cBaoding Department§! - §mLiu Faction§!\n§YBackground:§! §LLiu Wenhui has been in the army his whole life thanks to his brother enrolling him into the Sichuanese Military Academy in 1911. Most notably, it was under his watch, parts of western Xikang were lost to Tibet. Despite the fact that his early military record is marred by poor results, his connections within the Baoding Department and relative popularity in the cities has made him a force to be reckoned with.§!

§YBorn:§! §L1886, in Renshou County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §cBaoding Department§! - §mLiu Faction§!\n§YBackground:§! §LJoining the Tongmenghui in 1902 and engaging in revolutionary circles, Xiang Chuanyi was active in Sichuan even in the years before Xinhai, joining the ranks of Xiong Kewu when the revolution came. Serving under the revolutionary coalition, Xiang would come into contact with regional militarists Deng Xihou and Tian Songyao while also finding himself aligning more with the Industrial Corps over Xiong. Xiong Kewu's expulsion from Sichuan, first by Yunnan-Guizhou forces, then by the other warlords of Sichuan, would result in Xiang working with the nascent Baoding Department.\n\nDe jure a subordinate to Liu Wenhui, in practice, Xiang would play the role of an autonomous actor merely privy to Baoding interests. It would be this dynamic that resulted in Xiang Chuanyi being appointed as head of the Three Army Command Office in 1925, when the Baoding Department controlled Chengdu, a method of compromise for the triumvirate jostling for power. In this role, Xiang was responsible for dialogue between Liu, Deng and Tian while also unofficially being the police chief and head of the militia. While the Command Office has since been relocated to Ya'an due to hostilities with Yang Sen, Xiang's duties have remained mostly the same.§!

Liu Cunhou
§YBorn:§! §LJanuary 24th, 1884, in Jianzhou, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YBackground:§! §LThe son of wealthy salt merchants, Liu Cunhou is more of a brutish bandit than a strategist, let alone a governor. He attended Sichuan's military academy in 1903, went on to study in Japan in 1907, and returned to Sichuan after Graduating in 1908. He made a name for himself during the Xinhai Revolution where he initially led smaller anti-bandit forces and went on to lead larger forces as his loyalty earned him the trust of his superiors. During the conflicts he participated in, however, his forces were often more concerned with extorting wealth from the locals than functioning as a proper military force. While his soldiers often behaved like bandits, he's managed to strong-arm himself into a position of relative power in Sichuan in the process, though he's hardly endeared himself to the people.\n\nDuring the Sichuanese civil war, Liu Cunhou hardly contributed to the Armament Department's breakthroughs, though his forces were plentiful enough to exploit the ones that were created during the conflict. As he was mostly experienced in anti-banditry operations, his forces spent most of the war flushing out Kuomintang loyalists and mopping up routed remnants of Liu Xiang's faction of the Accelerated Department. Thanks to this, his forces were left in a relatively unscathed state compared to the rest of the Armament Department, eventually allowing him to outmanoeuvre his supposed ally Yang Sen once the dust of the war had settled.\n\nHis reign as a governor of Sichuan would end up being little more than his de-facto banditry on a larger scale. Imposing countless of taxes upon the people and using his army to extort the rich and poor of Sichuan alike, he quickly grew to become a hated tyrant that chiefly managed to maintain power through violent repression and shameless bribery.§!
§YBorn:§! §LJanuary 24th, 1884, in Jianzhou, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §PArmament§!\n§YBackground:§! §LLiu Cunhou was enrolled into the Sichuanese Military Academy in 1903. He went on to study at the Japanese Imperial Army Academy in 1907 and had graduated by 1908, after which he returned to China. His military career began in proper during the Xinhai Revolution when he was first promoted to Lieutenant-General by the Beiyang commanders. He went on to lead a series of anti-bandit raids and would earn the trust of his superiors, enough so that he'd eventually go on to lead provisional armies. It is, however, speculated that this had less to do with his abilities as a commander and was rather a question of him seeming loyal in a time of conflict and chaos.\n\nWhile he was able to successfully pacify bandits and earn the trust of his superiors, he earned nothing but disdain from the locals as his armies would behave no better than the bandits they were supposed to fight. Wherever his forces went, they would effectively run a protection racket against the locals. Greedy and less concern with discipline than military doctrine, he's held in poor regard by most of his peers and was seen as a man largely devoid of military acumen through most of his career. Be that as it may, he was still the epitome of a ruthless warlord and managed to amass wealth and a powerbase to be reckoned with.\n\nHe was eventually subordinated to Yang Sen and tasked with maintaining order in the north of the country. His previous loyalty to the Zhili has earned him a high position within the Armament Department despite his spotty military record.§!

Yang Sen
§YBorn:§! §LFebruary 20th, 1884, in Guang'an, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YBackground:§! §LAfter entering the army in 1904, Yang Sen had his first brushes with politics when he joined the Tongmenghui Society while attending the Sichuan Education Hall in 1906. After graduating in 1908 he went on to serve in the Newly Created Army of the Great Qing until 1911. His background and connections allowed him to gain prominence when the Xinhai Revolution occurred. While serving under Cai E, he was eventually promoted to the rank of general in 1921 and posted in Chongqing. This pattern of rising in the ranks through political ties and manoeuvring more than military accomplishments went on when Yang Sen became an ally of Wu Peifu in 1925. When the Zhili became the dominant clique in the fully restored Great Qing, Yang Sen was elevated to the governorship of Sichuan.\n\nAs governor of Sichuan, Yang Sen stands out from other warlords with his platform of rapid modernisation both in the social and economic spheres, spearheaded with various infrastructure and educational projects that have improved the lives for many in Sichuan. While he is a competent enough military leader, the fact that Yang Sen owes much of his success to dealmaking and supporting the right people at the right time has put him and the Armament Department in a precarious position. His position is further undermined by his own lifestyle contradicting what he claims to be best for Sichuan, which has limited his public support. For instance, he would earn the ire of more conservative elements of Sichuan by speaking out against traditional roles for women and practices such as foot binding, and would then squander any goodwill he might have gotten from reformists by having eight concubines of his own and as many as thirty children between them.\n\nDespite his many imperfections, he's managed to hold on to power thus far with enough support both from the army and common folk to be a force to be reckoned with. And as long as he's in charge, the Zhili will have a reliable ally in Chengdu.§!
§YBorn:§! §LFebruary 20th, 1884, in Guang'an, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §aAccelerated Department§! - §aYang Sen§!\n§YBackground:§! §LYang Sen joined the army in 1904 and attended Sichuan's military academy which he graduated from in 1908. Afterwards, he served in the Qing New Army until 1911. His background and education allowed him to rise to prominence in Sichuan's military clique when the Xinhai Revolution began the same year. During this period Yang served under general Cai E, who also promoted him to become one of Sichuan's generals in 1921. After being posted to command the forces of Chongqing, Yang Sen would go on feud with the province's other warlords, most notably Xiong Kewu and Liu Xiang. Despite his attempts to undermine his opposition, he was never fully successful.\n\nHis position as military governor of Sichuan has far more to do with his ties to Wu Peifu and the Zhili clique than any military accomplishments. That being said, he's hardly an inept military leader either by any stretch of the imagination, having performed adequately in prior wars and maintained the Armament Department's prominence in Sichuan throughout his time as governor.§!

Liu Xiang
§YBorn:§! §LJuly 1st, 1888, in Dayi County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YBackground:§! §LLiu Xiang initially rose to prominence in 1920 when he helped lead Sichuan's forces to a victory over Yunnan. He rose through the ranks and would eventually lead a large contingent of Sichuanese forces against the Kuomintang during the Northern Expedition. During this conflict he managed to gain a reputation as a skilled, albeit reckless, commander with numerous victories to his name. Once the Northern Expedition had been soundly defeated, he returned to Sichuan and was assigned to Chongqing as the head of the city's garrison.\n\nWhile his performance during this conflict allowed him to establish his own clique and remain a force to be reckoned with within Sichuan, he was never granted the governorship of the province that he felt that he deserved. Instead, he was made a subordinate to Yang Sen, a loyal stooge of Wu Peifu and the Zhili. Liu Xiang saw this as an insult and petty cronyism. As a result, he began to harbour a deep resentment towards both Yang Sen and the Zhili and their puppets in the Armament Department.\n\nThis resentment eventually led to him seizing the moment after the collapse of the Qing hegemony in the early days of 1936. After successfully rallying his subordinates in the Accelerated Department against Yang Sen, Liu Xiang managed to claim Chengdu for himself and defeat both the Armament and Baoding departments through a series of daring military gambles during the Sichuanese Civil War. After making himself the governor of Sichuan and moving the province's capital from Chengdu to the industrial powerhouse of Chongqing, he set out to rapidly modernise the region, hoping to eventually settle his scores with the Zhili.§!

§YBorn:§! §LOctober 16th, 1886, in Renshou County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §aAccelerated Department§! - §aFucheng Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LLeaving his struggling family to find ways to make ends meet in Chengdu, Pan Wenhua found irregular employment as an apprentice at a hat shop before being advised to join the Qing New Army. §YBorn:§! §LApril 14th, 1893, in Chongqing, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §aAccelerated Department§! - §aFucheng Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LDropping out of school with excellent grades after completing primary due to poor finances, Lu Zuofu joined the Tongmenghui in 1910, and participated in the Sichuan Railway Protection Movement. In 1913 Lu participated in the Second Revolution, and with its failure left Chengdu for Shanghai. The following year, he met Huang Yanpei and established a deep friendship with him. Returning to Chonqging to work as a mathematics teacher, he became an editor for the Chengdu Qunbao and Chuanbao, writing many articles criticising societal problems.\n\nIn 1919 he took over as president and editor-in-chief of the Sichuan Daily, actively participating in the November Riots, and even joining the Young China Society organised by Li Dazhao. In 1921, he became the head of the Education Section of the Yongning Daoyin Office in Sichuan, carrying out various mass education schemes, albeit aborted due to Warlord infighting. In 1924, Lu was invited by Yang Sen to Chengdu to establish the Popular Education Centre and partake in various reformist efforts.\n\nIn 1925, after his experiments with education failed, Lu transitioned to industry, setting up the Minsheng Industrial Company. In 1926, after personally raising capital, he started his transportation career, and established a variety of different infrastructure projects all-throughout the Yangtze. In 1927 he was appointed by Liu Xiang to the Sichuan provincial Government, and by 1935 had become cemented as one of China's most influential shipping magnates.§!
Liu Hangchen
§YBorn:§! §L1897, in Lu County, Sichuan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §aAccelerated Department§! - §aFucheng Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LBorn under a Catholic distillery operator, Liu Hangchen enrolled in the Department of Economics at Beijing University in 1917. After graduating in 1922, Liu returned to Lu County and became principal of Lu County Middle School. In 1926, after a scuffle with a local bureaucrat, Liu vowed to enter politics, and the following year, Liu was recommended by classmate Chen Xuechi (then a subordinate of Wang Lingji) to become director of the Chongqing Copper Coin Bureau.\n\nConducting various monetary reforms, Liu earned the admiration of Wang Lingji, and was thus recommended to Liu Xiang to become section chief of his army's finance department, wherein he soon became director. Departing from usual warlord methods, Liu reformed and streamlined local taxes, and eventually established a monopoly on opium under Liu Xiang's control.\n\nIn 1930, Liu proposed the establishment of the Army's General Treasury, which he utilised to issue effective government bonds, and treasury bills.
